Question no 32 by Eija-Riitta Anneli Korhola (H-0668/01)
 Subject: Fire protection in low-floor buses
 

Experience in Finland has shown that fire protection in city buses is inadequate. There are particular problems with ‘low-floor buses’, in which the engine space is cramped and very well insulated, which means that the temperature, particularly in summer, may rise above the danger level. On the other hand, large numbers of disabled people travel on these buses in particular, as do people travelling with prams and buggies, which means that it is hard to evacuate the passengers quickly. It is easy and cheap, in relation to the cost of the vehicle and the risk involved, to equip the engine space with an automatic fire extinguishing system.

Is there any information available from other Member States about fire safety in buses, particularly low-floor buses? Does the Presidency propose to take any action to implement common fire safety provisions in the Community? On what timescale will it be possible to bring the passenger safety of low-floor buses up to an acceptable level?

 
  
 

During Council discussions on the draft directive on special provisions applicable to buses and coaches, the Commission stated its intention of considering the results of the regulatory work carried out by the United Nations Economic Commission for Europe (UNECE) regarding low-floor buses, and of proposing any amendments to the directive in question if necessary.

The Council welcomes this undertaking on the part of the Commission, and will be ready to consider any proposed amendments resulting from the work of UNECE.

The Council and the Commission have made a statement on the need to take measures "as quickly as possible" to improve fire protection, and that statement has been communicated to the European Parliament. Such measures would have to be based on proposals from the Commission.

As far as the directive itself is concerned, I welcome the work that has been done by our two institutions, which made it easier for the Conciliation Committee to agree on a text at its meeting on 25 June, and I should like to express the hope that this directive will be formally adopted and published as soon as possible.

 

Question no 33 by Olivier Dupuis (H-0677/01)
 Subject: Death penalty
 

In its resolution of 5 July 2001(1) Parliament 'commits the Belgian Presidency of the Council to reintroducing and this time bringing to a vote in the United Nations General Assembly of September 2001 a resolution on the abolition of the death penalty and to taking all steps necessary to obtain its adoption'. Given that 125 UN member countries now support abolition to varying degrees – and not 110 as certain NGOs suggest – a resolution aimed at establishing a universal moratorium on executions as a first step towards the universal abolition of the death penalty would have every chance of being adopted by the UN General Assembly provided that the EU acts decisively and coherently, whilst at the same time demonstrating openness and flexibility towards third countries.

Has the Council Presidency already asked or will it ask, on behalf of the 15 Member States, that the introduction of a universal moratorium on executions be entered on the agenda for the forthcoming United Nations General Assembly? What steps has it already taken to ensure that the Union speaks with one voice and with the determination necessary for this initiative to succeed?

 
  
 

The Council reminds the Honourable Member of the content of the declaration on the abolition of the death penalty which was annexed to the Amsterdam Final Act. The European Union is opposed to the death penalty whatever the circumstances and has agreed to work towards its universal abolition. The EU is concerned by the fact that about 90 states retain the death penalty, although only a minority (30 countries) carry out executions each year.

In the context of its objective of universal abolition, in 1998 the European Union set out a series of guidelines to determine the circumstances in which it should take specific targeted action. In order to obtain the principal objective, which is the definitive abolition of the death penalty, the European Union attempts, in countries where the death penalty still exists, to limit its use progressively, and it insists that the penalty is carried out in a manner that observes certain minimum international standards. The European Union’s efforts to achieve the abolition of the death penalty are the expression of a deep conviction shared by all the Member States of the European Union. The abolition of the death penalty helps to improve respect for human rights.

Moreover, the Council reminds the Honourable Member of the European Union’s memorandum on the death penalty, issued in 2000 and included in the annual report on human rights in 2000, and of the policy guidelines of the European Union regarding third countries in relation to this subject.

By virtue of these guidelines, the European Union also raises the question of the death penalty within multilateral bodies. Thus in 1999, 2000 and 2001 the European Union succeeded in having a resolution against the death penalty adopted by the Human Rights Commission. That resolution contains, amongst other things, a request to those states that still use the death penalty to limit its use progressively before abolishing it completely and, while they continue to use it, to observe certain fundamental criteria.

At the 54th United Nations General Assembly in 1999, the European Union attempted, for the first time, to have a resolution on the death penalty adopted which incorporated the wording of the text adopted on the Union’s initiative by the Human Rights Commission. Despite gaining a large measure of support (72 countries cosponsored the motion), the European motion for a resolution came up against strong opposition from those countries in favour of retaining the death penalty, which were determined that the text include a series of amendments that would to some extent have rendered it meaningless. At the time, this led the European Union not to pursue its initiative within the General Assembly.

In 2000, after having considered the timeliness of a European initiative on the death penalty at the 55th United Nations General Assembly, the European Union found that the political and diplomatic climate did not yet make it possible to remove the obstacles that had arisen at the 54th United Nations General Assembly. In those circumstances, it appeared that to adopt a resolution which might have constituted a step backwards, in comparison with what had been achieved by the Human Rights Commission resolution, would be unthinkable. Therefore the European Union took the decision not to submit its resolution.

As far as the 56th United Nations General Assembly is concerned, no decision has yet been taken. The European Union’s experts are continuing to examine all the elements relating to a possible initiative against the death penalty. That assessment will take into account, in particular, the difficulties encountered during the first half of 2001 in the context of the negotiation process, in particular on HIV/AIDS, racism and the treatment of children, and the current events which are threatening to overshadow the work of the United Nations General Assembly.

 
 

(1) Texts adopted of 5.7.2001.

 

Question no 34 by Harlem Désir (H-0680/01)
 Subject: Placing the subject of taxation of international financial transactions on the agenda for the informal ECOFIN Council to be held in Liège on 22-23 September 2001
 

Can the Presidency confirm that it intends to put the question of a tax on international financial transactions (along the lines of the Tobin tax) on the agenda for the informal Council of Finance Ministers to be held in Liège on 22-23 September 2001?

Would the Presidency be prepared to provide the Council with studies giving different points of view, including those of economists who favour a tax of this kind? Is the Presidency aware, for example, of the proposal for a two-tier tax developed in recent years by Professor Paul Bernd Spahn of the Goethe University in Frankfurt, a former IMF consultant?

 
  
 

The Presidency informs the Honourable Member that the Finance Ministers, at the informal Council to be held on 22-23 September, intend to hold an in-depth discussion on the advisability of introducing a tax on speculative currency movements at European level.

With regard to the proposals of Professor Bernd Spahn, the Presidency is aware of those proposals, since they were submitted to the Finance Committee of the Belgian Senate in June 2000. Those proposals, and other approaches, will provide a contribution to our reflections on the taxing of speculative movements.

 

Question no 35 by Antonios Trakatellis (H-0683/01)
 Subject: Reconstruction of the Balkans and an enhanced role for the European Agency for Reconstruction
 

Given that the reconstruction of the Balkans requires substantial economic aid in the form of investments and financial aid for the execution of projects and assistance for the development of the institutional, legislative and economic framework, will the Council say:

How much financial aid did the Member States give to the countries of southeastern Europe in 1999 and 2000 and how much has Greece given since 1999 for the economic reconstruction of countries in this region? The European Agency for Reconstruction based in Thessaloniki has been downgraded: how many staff currently work there and what is its real function? How can its role be enhanced in order to improve the management and execution of projects carried out in the countries eligible under Regulation (EC) 2667/00(1)?

 
  
 

Community aid to the countries of south-eastern Europe which are involved in the process of stabilisation and association, that is to say Albania, Bosnia-Herzegovina, Croatia, the Federal Republic of Yugoslavia and the Former Yugoslavian Republic of Macedonia, amounts to EUR 4.65 billion for the period 2000-2006. This aid is provided, essentially, in the context of the CARDS programme launched in December 2000, which follows on from the previous programmes for the region, OBNOVA and PHARE.1

The CARDS programme covers very different types of aid, including aid for recovery and reconstruction after a conflict. The European Agency for Reconstruction was created after the conflict in Kosovo, and initially it concentrated its efforts on the reconstruction of Kosovo. Since then its activities have been extended to cover the rest of the Federal Republic of Yugoslavia, that is, Montenegro and Serbia2. The implementation of Community assistance to the whole of the Federal Republic of Yugoslavia under the CARDS programme was therefore delegated to the Agency, whose activities played a very vital role, as was shown in a report from the Commission to the Parliament and the Council in July this year.3

With regard to the exact amounts of aid provided by Member States of the EU to south-eastern Europe in 1999 and 2000, and in particular by Greece, the Council suggests that the Honourable Member contact the countries in question direct, since the Council does not systematically collect or process information on bilateral aid provided by Member States. The question referring to the staffing of the Agency should be put directly to the Commission.

 
 

(1) OJ L 306, 7.12.2000, p. 7.

 

Question no 36 by Richard Howitt (H-0684/01)
 Subject: The EU contribution to the Global Health Fund
 

Given the conclusions of the Development Council held on 31 May 2001 that: ‘The Council and the Commission note with concern the devastating impact of HIV/AIDS and other communicable diseases on human suffering and on economic and social development, and hence also on poverty reduction efforts. The proposal by the UN Secretary-General to establish a global HIV/AIDS and Health Fund is therefore fully welcomed. The Commission and Member States will explore how best to develop this initiative’, can the President-in-Office outline where such additional monies may be found at the EU level? Specifically does this include exceeding the ceiling of Category Four of the budget, transferring money from other chapters within the budget, or allocating some of the underspent monies from the budget overall due to be returned to Member States?

 
  
 

The Council draws the attention of the honourable Member to the fact that the implementation of the budget is a matter for the Commission and that a legal basis is required for the spending of appropriations. The statement quoted cannot be regarded as a legal basis. As a result, at this stage, there is no legal basis for a contribution from the Community to the Global Aids and Health Fund. No Commission proposal has yet been referred to the Council on this matter. The Council will state a position on the amounts and sources of funding for the Community contribution on the basis of such a proposal. The Council has no doubt that the Commission proposal will fully comply with the provisions of the Interinstitutional Agreement of 6 May 1999.

 

QUESTIONS TO THE COMMISSION
Question no 55 by Rodi Kratsa-Tsagaropoulou (H-0647/01)
 Subject: Fresh impetus for tourism policy
 

Further to the ministerial meeting on tourism held in Bruges on 1 and 2 July 2001, and pending the communication entitled 'Framing the Future for European Tourism', does the Commission consider it feasible to apply the open coordination method to tourism policy and what would be the substance thereof? What role might the European Parliament play in drawing up that policy? Does the Commission intend to establish a dialogue with representatives of the tourism and recreation sector in drawing up a cohesive strategy for tourism? Will the Commission reactivate the process for the approval of a multiannual action programme for tourism? What initiatives will the Commission take to promote social tourism, in particular, within the European programmes?

 
  
 

The European Ministerial Conference “Tourism for All” was held during the Belgian Presidency in Bruges, on 1 and 2 July 2001. On that occasion the Commission submitted the final recommendations of the five working parties set up following the conclusions of the Council of 21 June 1999 on tourism and employment.

The Commission’s objective is now to adopt the communication “Framing the Future for European Tourism: a Strategic and Cooperative Approach” and, in November 2001, to send it to the Council, the Parliament, the Economic and Social Committee and the Committee of the Regions. This communication will propose, in close consultation with all the parties involved, a contextual and strategic framework for tourism activities and policies within the Union.

The Commission intends to use the open coordination method proposed at the European Council in Lisbon in March 2000, so as to facilitate the convergence of Member States’ policies on tourism and the coordination of policies and measures which have an impact on tourism. Representatives from the various tourism sectors will be consulted. An initial joint meeting between representatives of the industry, other operators in civil society and Member States is planned to take place on 5 September 2001.

Parliament ought to play a full role here, as it did in 1999, through its Committee on Regional Policy, Transport and Tourism, in the case of the communication on increasing the potential of tourism for employment. The Commission is ready to submit to it the new communication and to respond positively and actively to any other request from Parliament that would help to clarify the proposals that will be formulated.

Since discussions on the working parties’ recommendations have not yet been completed, it is too soon, at present, to give any commitments regarding the Commission’s final proposals. The following basic principles will form the basis for those proposals:

- ensuring better coordination of the whole of the sector, by means of structured mechanisms for consultation, exchanges and information,

- improving knowledge of the sector at economic, social and environmental level,

- building on existing structure, bodies and centres of competence and knowledge, whilst respecting the principle of subsidiarity and encouraging access by means of networking.

The social aspect is, of course, well to the fore in these reflections, both for tourism professionals (working conditions, training, career prospects) and for tourists themselves (the disabled, the unemployed and people on low incomes).

 

Question no 56 by Minerva Melpomeni Malliori (H-0654/01)
 Subject: Presence of inedible objects in food
 

Reputable studies show that children up to 14 years of age have been seriously injured and in some cases have died as a result of swallowing inedible objects (toy components) found in food.

Has the Commission conducted any research into this matter in line with the commitment given by Commissioner Liikanen ten months ago when he was presented with the relevant information? Does it consider that Directive 88/378/EEC(1) on the safety of toys covers this particular contingency (the toy in this case is not immediately visible, as the Directive clearly intended) and, if not, does it intend to take the necessary supplementary measures as a matter of urgency in compliance with the precautionary principle? Does it consider that, on the basis of the precautionary principle, the Member States may make national legislative arrangements to safeguard the lives and health of children until such time as this issue is regulated at Community level? If so, under what conditions may they do so?

 
  
 

The issues raised by the Honourable Member are both complex and controversial. The Commission is fully aware of the Honourable Members concerns on the safety aspects of the association of small toys and food, and of the view that the situation is not adequately addressed by existing rules. It is aware also of opposite views within the Parliament, which insist that the existing rules are adequate to address any potential risk, and that any further measures would be disproportionate.

The Commission's approach will be guided by the Treaty obligation to take, as a base for any measures which may be proposed, a high level of protection. This means that, on the one hand where measures are objectively justified to protect consumers it shall act without reserve, and on the other hand it will fulfil its Treaty obligation to ensure the free movement of goods. This means also that it will not agree to the introduction of measures at the national level which cannot be properly justified.

In a very comprehensive reply of 19 October 2000 to a series of Questions on this subject, the Commission explained the legal and administrative aspects in relation to the combination of non-food products with foodstuffs. On the specific issue of accidents involving children, it outlined the results of its investigation carried out with the assistance of the Emergencies Committee set up under article 8 of Directive 92/59/EEC on general product safety.

 
 

(1) OJ L 187, 16.7.1988, p.1.

 

Question no 57 by Camilo Nogueira Román (H-0596/01)
 Subject: Fishing in Community waters for the purposes of fish-meal production
 

Has the Commission considered the possibly disastrous effects which the catching of fish by Community vessels (particularly the Danish fleet) for processing into fish meal is having on the conservation of fisheries resources?

 
  
 

Two type of effects of the industrial fisheries are discussed: the direct effect on the target and by-catch species and the indirect effects on the ecosystem caused by the removal of large quantities of the target species, which are known to be important prey for a large number of animals.

The impacts on target species and commercially important by-catch species are assessed annually by the International Council for the Exploration of the Sea (ICES). According to ICES the industrial fisheries are sustainable both in relation to the target species as well as the by-catch species.

Regarding the indirect impacts of fisheries on the ecosystem the Commission is unaware of definitive scientific results which indicate that current catch levels in the industrial fisheries have created deleterious effects. The only exception to this position concerns an apparent lack of breeding success of a species of seabird off the east coast of Scotland. In response to this, fisheries for sandeels were closed in the appropriate area of the North Sea.

The Community already has in place measures to control the activities of vessels conducting industrial fisheries. These are mainly held in Regulation EC N° 850/98 for the conservation of fishery resources through technical measures for the protection of juveniles of marine organisms and, in particular, Annexes I and II thereof.

 

Question no 58 by Pedro Aparicio Sánchez (H-0598/01)
 Subject: Requirement for Spanish nationality for Iberia pilots
 

Article 39 of the Treaty prohibits discrimination against Community workers on grounds of nationality. Despite this fact, the Spanish Government prevents Iberia from employing pilots who do not hold Spanish nationality. It justifies this restriction by claiming that some of the duties performed by pilots (authorisation to perform marriage for persons on board whose lives are in danger, registration of births, safekeeping of diplomatic bags, etc.) give them the status of an 'authority', an interpretation which, in my opinion, is not only archaic, but also incompatible with Community law.

In the Commission's opinion, does the requirement for Spanish nationality for Iberia pilots constitute a breach of the EC Treaty? Do other Member States impose this legal constraint? If the answer to the first question is affirmative, does the Commission intend to oblige the governments concerned to rectify the situation?

 
  
 

Under Community law (Article 39 paragraph 4 of the EC Treaty) Member States are allowed to reserve posts for their own nationals which involve the exercise of public authority, particularly the maintenance of public order, public security and the respect of laws and regulations.

Although the Spanish authorities previously relied on this principle to reserve aircraft commander posts for their own nationals, they modified "Ley 48/1960, de 21 de julio, de Navegación Aérea" [Law 48/1960, of 21 July, on Air Navigation] on 17 April 1999 and now also allow nationals of other Member States to become captain and first officer of an aeroplane.

Based on the information available to the Commission, no other Member State has introduced such nationality-based restrictions on access to the occupation of pilot in the air transport sector.

 

Question no 59 by James (Jim) Fitzsimons (H-0610/01)
 Subject: Legal action against Sellafield
 

As the Commission is no doubt aware, the UK faces the prospect of legal action over Sellafield following an official warning that Ireland could face a Chernobyl-type disaster at the Sellafield plant, and because the UK has refused to release vital technical information on the grounds of confidentiality about a proposed £300 million mixed oxide (MOX) nuclear reprocessing plant in Sellafield. Is the Commission in possession of this information and, if so, under the rules recently agreed by the European Parliament, the Council and the Commission on public access to documents, will the Commission release this information and, if it is in possession of this information but will not grant access now, or in relation to future requests, will it justify its reasons for doing so as required under the agreement?

 
  
 

The Commission is not aware of an official warning as referred to by the Honourable Member.

If the Honourable Member gives further details of the possible legal action to which he refers and of the technical information on the mixed oxide (MOX) fuel fabrication facility which he indicates British Nuclear Fuels PLC (BNFL) has refused to release, the Commission will be in a better position to determine whether it is in possession of any of the information.

Any request for access to documents lodged with the Commission would be handled in accordance with the currently applicable rules in this area, that is, Commission Decision 94/90(1) 1, of 8 February 1994, on public access to Commission documents.

Regulation (EC) N° 1049/2001(2) 2 of the Parliament and of the Council, of 30 May 2001, regarding public access to Parliament, Council and Commission documents will be applicable from December 2001 onwards.

 
 

(1)1 O.J.E.C. N° L 46, of 18.2.1994, p. 46
(2)2 O.J.E.C. N° L 145, of 31.5.2001, p. 43

 

Question no 60 by Nuala Ahern (H-0670/01)
 Subject: Monitoring mission to Sellafield
 

What plans does the Commission have to send an environmental and radiological monitoring mission to the UK nuclear complex at Sellafield in light of the admission by BNFL, in an internal report leaked to the press in July 2001, that radioactive emissions have risen rather than been reduced since the UK signed the OSPAR Convention to reduce discharges in Sintra, Portugal, in July 1998?

 
  
 

The Commission refers to the reply given to Oral Question H-0543/01 during Question time at Parliament's July 2001(1) 1 session. The Commission has not planned an environmental radioactivity monitoring verification visit to Sellafield in the near future.

A commitment to reducing discharges, emissions and losses of radioactive substances by the year 2020 as part of the Sintra Statement (OSPAR, 1998) was signed by the British Government. The Commission is a contracting party to OSPAR but it is for the British Government to decide on the appropriate strategy for the implementation of the requirement for progressive reduction in discharges. It is not within the remit of the verifications under Article 35 of the Euratom Treaty to check the implementation of the Sintra commitment nor to conduct other verifications in that context.

The Commission has launched a major study on levels of radioactivity in North European marine waters (MARINA II) which will serve as input to OSPAR for achieving its objectives. The study will look into concentrations of radioactive substances and doses to man as well as the possible impact on biota.

The Commission is also preparing a report on the radioactive effluents from 1995 to 1999 from major nuclear installations in the Union using data transmitted by Member States under Commission Recommendations 91/4/EURATOM(2) 2 and 99/829/EURATOM(3) 3 on the application of Article 37 of the EURATOM Treaty. Furthermore the Commission is currently financing a project on doses to members of the public resulting from those effluents.

 
 

(1)1 Written Reply, 3.7.2001
(2)2 OJ L 6 of 9.1.1991, p. 16
(3)3 OJ L 324 of 16.12.1999, p. 23

 

Question no 61 by Ioannis Marinos (H-0615/01)
 Subject: EUROSTAT data on the environment in Greece
 

According to a recent EUROSTAT bulletin (No 62/2001 of 7 June 2001), energy consumption in Greece rose by 47% between 1985 and 1998, compared with an average increase of 16% in the EU as a whole. The Eurostat bulletin also contains many other worrying figures on air pollution in Greece: sulphur dioxide emissions in 1998, for example, were eight times higher in Greece than in Austria or Sweden. EUROSTAT puts Greece and the United Kingdom at the bottom of the EU league for glass recycling, with a recycling rate of only 26% compared with 88% in Austria. The situation is similar with respect to paper recycling, where Greece comes second last with 29% above Ireland with 12%. What is the Commission's view of the standard reached in Greece as regards measures to protect the atmosphere? Has it taken any concrete steps to persuade the Greek authorities to set up an integrated programme to cut energy consumption and the resulting environmental pollution and exploit the vast possibilities offered by waste recycling?

 
  
 

The long-term goal of the Community is a reduction of the energy intensity (energy consumption per gross domestic product (GDP) created) by 1% per year. A rapid implementation of measures launched or proposed by the Commission on, inter alia, co-generation, energy performance of building and efficiency requirements for electronic apparatus would help to achieve this goal and to reduce the growth in energy consumption in all Member States. Under the so-called "Burden Sharing Agreement" decided upon in Council in June 1998 in regard to the Kyoto Protocol, Greece agreed to limit its greenhouse gas emissions to a 25 % increase by 2008-2012, from 1990 levels. The first report on Community greenhouse gas emissions monitoring in 2000 notes an expected rise in direct CO2 emissions of 28.8% in Greece from 1990 to 2008/12. No data have been reported on the other five Kyoto Protocol greenhouse gases. Greece has provided a list of policies and measures that cover most of the relevant sectors and sources, but has not quantified the expected effects. New projections incorporating the latest list of policies and measures would be useful in assessing the extent to which Greece is likely to meet its commitment.

The new directive on large combustion plants whose final adoption is expected later this year will apply new requirements for older plants from 1 January 2008 which will substantially reduce emissions of sulphur dioxide in particular from the energy sector in Greece. Implementation of the related proposal on national emission ceilings and new air quality limit values will also reduce levels of sulphur dioxide and nitrogen dioxide.

Where waste recycling is concerned, the Court of Justice ruled against Greece in its judgement of 13 April 2000 (Case C-99/123) on the grounds that Greece had failed to adopt implementing legislation to incorporate the Packaging Waste Directive into its national laws by the stipulated deadline of 30 June 1996. The Greek authorities indicated that new legislation is being prepared. This was initially announced for March 2001. However, to date, no adopted legislative text has been communicated to the Commission.

 

Question no 62 by Manuel Medina Ortega (H-0618/01)
 Subject: European Union representation in the Republic of Ecuador
 

Does the Commission intend to open an office to represent the European Union in Ecuador and, if so, when?

 
  
 

The Commission has recently approved a Communication to the Council and the Parliament on the evolution of the External Service.

Following a review of its priorities the Commission plans to open limited number of new representations by the end of 2002. A delegation in Ecuador is not on that list.

Decisions on openings were taken based on an analysis of Community interests in the regions where the Commission is still underrepresented. Apart from general political considerations, factors such as the ever more important trade component as well as the implementation of assistance were essential to the considerations.

Given the tight financial conditions under which the External Service operates the Commission has had to prepare a budget plan which was neutral. In order to be able to open elsewhere, difficult decisions have had to be taken concerning the closure or downsizing of a number of Delegations.

Under the circumstances it has proved impossible to open in all the countries where the Commission might have wished to at this stage.

 

Question no 63 by María Izquierdo Rojo (H-0622/01)
 Subject: Withdrawal of olive residue oil
 

As a precautionary measure, all sales of olive residue oil have been stopped in Spain and it has been withdrawn from shops as a matter of urgency. Does this oil comply with European rules on olive residue oil? Has there been accidental contamination or is the problem a consequence of the normal manufacturing process? Was it the return of a shipment of oil exported to the Czech Republic which revealed the excessive level of benzopyrene? What other shipments have been exported and to which countries? Was the existence of this shipment known of in May of this year? Will compensation be provided for the damage caused? What will be done with the oil which has been withdrawn?

 
 

Question no 64 by María Rodríguez Ramos (H-0631/01)
 Subject: Benzopyrene and olive residue oil
 

The use of the early warning system by the Spanish authorities to warn of the presence of benzopyrene in olive residue oil has caused public alarm in both Spain and Europe. Some Member States have exercised their option of taking protective measures and have announced that they are suspending orders for all olive oil of Spanish origin, regardless of its appellation. Given that the Commission does not yet have at its disposal all the information it has requested from the Spanish authorities:

What does it think about the actions of one Member State, which has introduced the above measures without attempting at the outset to identify the batch or brand name of the product affected or to consider the analyses and scientific tests carried out in order to ascertain and assess the risks involved?

The Commission has recognised that polycyclic aromatic hydrocarbons are generated during the process of obtaining olive residue oil, but that they do not affect olive oil, where the production process does not contain this component.

What will the Commission do to protect Spanish olive oil producers, whose market and reputation are already suffering the adverse effects of a public health problem relating to a different product?

 
 

Question no 65 by María Sornosa Martínez (H-0633/01)
 Subject: Benzopyrene content in Spanish olive-residue oil
 

In view of the Spanish Ministry of Health’s ban on the sale of olive-residue oil and the consequences this has had for the sector, can the Commission say what progress has been made in its studies on benzopyrene and state its conclusions regarding the limit values it intends to propose?

Pending the legislation on benzopyrene, what provisional measures will the Commission take to ensure proper consumer protection, without jeopardising the olive-oil sector?

Joint Answer

On 3rd July 2001, as required by Community legislation, Spain informed the Commission’s Rapid Alert System for Food that it had withdrawn products from the market, in this case olive-residue oil contaminated with Polycyclic Aromatic Hydrocarbons (PAHs), such as benzo(a)pyrene. PAHs are known carcinogens.

In these circumstances the Commission must immediately issue a Rapid Alert notification to Member States and request the necessary further details. In this case Spain was asked for details of test results, and distribution to Member States and third countries. Ideally such information should be submitted with original notifications, but this is not always possible, particularly where a general contamination problem is being investigated. It is important to inform the Member States immediately when urgent information is received, so they can begin their own investigations and consider whether national action is necessary to protect consumers.

Contamination of Spanish oil was first identified by authorities in the Czech Republic, at the end of May 2001. However, details were initially unclear and the Spanish authorities made an investigation. Spain notified the Commission after tests confirmed that products were contaminated. This contamination concerned only olive-residue oil and was a result of the production process.

On 6 July, at a meeting of the Commission’s Expert Committee on Contaminants in Food, Spain presented data showing levels of PAHs in olive-residue oil many times higher than guideline levels used by the vegetable oil industry. Subsequent investigations in Spain identified the precise stages of production where the contamination arose. This process is now being changed. In addition, the investigations in Spain revealed details of the distribution of products to several Member States and third countries. All of these destinations have been informed via the Rapid Alert System.

No harmonised maximum levels exist for PAHs in food, but the general food safety provisions of the framework regulation on contaminants apply(1) 1. In such cases Member States may take action based upon national provisions to protect consumer safety. In this instance, Member States withdrew suspect products from the market. Spain offered to take back any contaminated olive-residue oil. Options on what to do with the contaminated oil are being considered, such as reprocessing or use in non-food products such as fuel oil.

The Scientific Committee on Food (SCF) made an urgent assessment and concluded that there were no immediate risks from the levels of PAHs found, but the long-term risks from regular exposure to such high levels in food might be unacceptable, particularly regarding the risk of cancer. Therefore contaminated foods must be withdrawn. The SCF will perform the assessment of long-term risk whilst Member States collect data on actual levels of PAHs in different foods. The outcome of these activities will help determine whether harmonised maximum levels need to be set.

 
 
 

(1)1 Council Regulation (EEC) N° 315/93 of 8 February 1993, laying down Community procedures for contaminants in food.

 

Question no 66 by Graham R. Watson (H-0625/01)
 Subject: Subsidies in the Czech banking industry
 

The Commission is presenting regular reports on convergence and approximation of applicant countries' legislative frameworks to the acquis. While some significant progress has been noted, the situation varies significantly among applicant countries and among different industries within each applicant country. During the period since it entered into its Europe Agreement, the Czech Republic has granted subsidies to its banking industry equal to approximately 20% of GDP, apparently without meaningful measures to avoid distortions to competition. This had led to the filing of a complaint with the competition service of the Commission.

What is the view of the Commission on the Czech Republic's record of compliance with the rules on State aid and competition in the banking industry?

 
  
 

The Commission has been following attentively the competition dimension of the various restructuring operations in the Czech financial sector, notably in the light of the Czech Republic’s obligations under the Europe Agreement. The Commission is of the view that greater transparency is first needed regarding these operations, before any conclusions can be drawn regarding compliance with the Europe Agreement. To this end the Commission is following up in detail these issues with the Czech authorities using fully the mechanisms of the Europe Agreement.

The overall track record of the Czech Republic in compliance with the competition acquis, including in the financial sector, is currently in the process of being assessed, as follows.

In the framework of the accession negotiations, the Commission is assessing for each candidate country whether the conditions are met that could allow for the provisional closure of the competition chapter. Three elements must be in place to reach that objective: (1) the necessary legislative framework with respect to antitrust and State aid; (2) an adequate administrative capacity; and (3) a credible enforcement record of the competition acquis. These three conditions, in particular the enforcement record, are currently being examined by the Commission with a view to forming its official position in the second half of the year, in line with the negotiation Road Map, agreed at the Nice European Council of December 2000.

The Commission last commented on the Czech Republic's overall progress in implementing the competition acquis in its Regular Report of November 2000. This concluded that alignment of Czech legislation on State aid was now well advanced, but that the main challenge was still to ensure the effective application and enforcement of State aid rules, where greater efforts were required. This year's Regular Report is currently under preparation and will be adopted in November.

 

Question no 67 by Anna Karamanou (H-0627/01)
 Subject: Involvement of children in armed conflict
 

According to the UN, 79 countries have signed the May 2000 Protocol calling on countries not to allow children to take part in armed conflict, but only 4 countries have ratified it. Moreover, the recent report by the Judit Arenas Coalition states that the use of child soldiers has assumed terrifying proportions as they now number more than 300 000 in 41 countries in the world. Youths and young children are used – even in the front line – as mine detectors, spies, sentries, porters etc.

Will the Commission, in the context of its relations with third countries, exert pressure on those countries to adopt and strictly implement the UN Protocol on the involvement of children in armed conflict?

 
  
 

The widespread use of child soldiers is a scourge, which the international community must fight with every available means. The Union's support for the United Nations (UN) Optional Protocol was reiterated recently at the UN Commission on Human Rights.

The Commission is deeply committed to relieving the plight of children affected by armed conflict. Assistance and protection of children in the midst of armed conflict is channelled through several Community instruments. One of these is the European Initiative for Human Rights and Democracy, which is currently preparing the funding of a major United Nations international children's emergency fund (UNICEF) project in Sierra Leone.

In addition, the European Commission Humanitarian Aid Office (ECHO) has made relief and support to children a priority this year - a priority which is likely to be continued in the years to come. Therefore, significant funding is dedicated to child-related assistance and protection in crisis theatres around the world.

In conclusion - as declared in the Commission Communication on Human Rights - in promoting human rights including child’s rights - the Union will seek to maximise the leverage afforded by the combination of political dialogue, trade and external assistance when engaging with third countries.

 

Question no 68 by Ioannis Patakis (H-0632/01)
 Subject: Subsidy fraud in Serres
 

The association of tomato producers in Serres has written to the Community officials responsible for carrying out checks on industrial tomatoes in the Serres region (Pilar Ramírez and Frederico Spanu) to complain that the companies, AXACO and ARGO, with the complicity of Greek government departments and the banks, made it appear that they had processed many times more than the actual amounts of industrial tomatoes in order to obtain Community subsidies illegally for the non-existent quantities.

Has the Commission investigated these allegations? What was the outcome of the investigation, and what punitive and preventive measures were taken?

 
  
 

Staff from the Commission’s agricultural expenditure audit service visited Greece in May 2001.

The aim of that visit was to inspect the control system set up by the Member State for Community production aid for the processing of tomatoes.

During their inspection visit to Serres, the auditors were given two letters addressed to the Commission from two tomato producers who stated that they were members of the Serres Producers’ Union.

Those two letters, which contained an accusation of fraud regarding the payment of the minimum price laid down by Community regulations, were passed on to the European Anti-Fraud Office (OLAF), and the two parties concerned were informed of this in writing. OLAF confirmed receipt of the accusation on 27 June 2001. This was assessed with a view to the appropriate measures being taken.

In addition, this case will be the subject of discussions, in the context of the final settlement of the accounts of the European Agricultural Guidance and Guarantee Fund (EAGGF), with the national authorities, with a view to assessing the weaknesses of the control system set up by the Member State.

 

Question no 69 by Ewa Hedkvist Petersen (H-0634/01)
 Subject: Corporal punishment of children and the Charter of Fundamental Rights
 

During the Gothenburg Summit, Save the Children organised a children's summit comprising children and young people from Sweden. The meeting reminded governments of their responsibility for the Convention on the Rights of the Child in their countries. The children's summit also considered that all children have the right not to be hit and that the EU should ensure that a total ban on corporal punishment of children is introduced in all Member States and applicant countries.

Does the Commission consider that corporal punishment of children is consistent with Article 24 of the Charter of Fundamental Rights?

 
  
 

The Commission noted with great interest and sympathy the conclusions reached by the children’s summit organised by the NGO “Save the Children”, to which the Honourable Member is referring.

It points out that, as indicated in the explanatory notes to the Charter of Fundamental Rights of the European Union (Doc. CONVENT 49 of 11 October 2000), Article 24 of that Charter is based on the New York Convention on the Rights of the Child, signed on 20 November 1989 and ratified by all Member States. Article 19 of that Convention requires all the signatory states to take appropriate measures to protect children against any form of physical violence. Such protection may also be derived from Article 24 of the Charter which stipulates, in paragraph 1, that children shall have the right to protection and to the care necessary for their well-being.

Such a principle should be respected by the Union, in accordance with the scope of the Charter which, under Article 51, is addressed to the institutions and bodies of the Union and to the Member States only when they are implementing Union law. Moreover, as far as candidate countries are concerned, respect for human rights, including the rights of the child, is one of the political criteria for accession laid down by the European Council in Copenhagen, and is examined by the Commission, in particular in the context of its regular contacts.

Question no 71 by Francisca Sauquillo Pérez del Arco (H-0649/01)
 Subject: Closure of the ECHO Office in Cuba
 

Is it true that the Commission plans to discontinue its humanitarian work in Cuba and to close the ECHO Office in Havana?

Does the Commission consider that the population of Cuba no longer needs humanitarian aid from the Commission?

Are relations with Cuba to be modified and, in this context, has the Commission made any provision for Cuba in its plans to restructure its External Service?

Joint Answer

The European Commission Humanitarian aid office (ECHO) in Cuba is to be closed in March as Community emergency assistance to Cuba is no longer needed. However, the Commission is putting equivalent funds into economic assistance which needs a different kind of presence. The Commission aims to make its co-operation with Cuba as efficient as possible. One should not read into this shift that the Commission is less attentive to the Government's human rights record.

The Commission has adopted a Communication to the Council and the Parliament on 3 July 2001. It foresees a number of new Delegations to be opened by the end of 2002. As far as Cuba is concerned the Communication sets out that a regular presence on the island is to be considered in the light of internal developments and the forthcoming closure of the ECHO office.

 
 

Question no 72 by Carmen Cerdeira Morterero (H-0644/01)
 Subject: The disabled and sea transport

Recently the company Transmediterranea introduced a new ship on the Ceuta-Algeciras route, which is still totally inaccessible for disabled people. Around the same time another shipping company acquired a new vessel which is accessible for the disabled, since it was built in Australia and that country makes it compulsory for shipping companies to ensure that their new vessels provide access for the disabled. Does the Commission envisage introducing any measures to oblige shipping companies to ensure that their vessels are accessible for the disabled? Is the Commission aware of the discrimination and hazards faced by disabled people in sea transport?

 
  
 

The Commission would like to refer to its reply to the honourable Member on her earlier question H-0030/01 answered by the Commission during Question Time of February 2001 plenary Session of Parliament and wish to clarify the following:

The Commission understands the problem faced by disabled people with their access to certain vessels operating in the Community ports, which do not have the appropriate facilities in that respect. Therefore, it will contribute positively to any efforts aiming to introduce construction and equipment standards in this field, at international level (International Maritime Organization (IMO)).

In addition the Commission has planed to present in the near future proposals to amend the existing Community legislation on safety of passenger vessels. In that framework, the Commission intends to examine possible solutions facilitating the access of the disabled to such vessels.

 

Question no 73 by Miquel Mayol i Raynal (H-0650/01)
 Subject: The Catalan language in the EU institutions
 

Catalan is spoken by eight million people. In Spain, it is the joint official language of the autonomous communities of CATALONIA, the BALEARIC Islands, VALENCIA (in the form of the Valencian dialect), and the Eastern part of ARAGON. Outside Spain, it is the autochthonous language of the Pyrénées Orientales department in France and the town of Alghero in Sardinia. It is also the official language of Andorra. Catalan is therefore spoken by more people, over a wider area, than several of the official languages of the European Union.

Given that respect for cultural and linguistic diversity is one of the fundamental principles of the Union, does the Commission consider the status accorded to Catalan within the Community institutions to be keeping with this principle? If it does not, how does it intend to go about improving the situation, particularly during the European Year of Languages?

 
  
 

In accordance with Article 290 of the EC Treaty, the Council adopted Regulation No 1 of 1958 (subsequently amended to take into account the accession of new members) determining the languages to be used by the European Economic Community. It laid down the official languages and the working languages of the institutions as being German, English, Danish, Spanish, Finnish, French, Greek, Italian, Dutch, Portuguese and Swedish.

This regulation, which is concerned with the operation of the institutions, in no way casts doubt upon the cultural and linguistic importance of languages other than those quoted, including Catalan. For its part, the Commission attaches great importance to the respecting of cultural and linguistic diversity in the Union, without considering it necessary to propose any amendments to Regulation 1/1958. In this area, the Commission does not have any specific power of initiative.

Moreover, the Community has undertaken many initiatives to promote cultural and linguistic diversity. A good example is the European Year of Languages, the aim of which is to make the public at large aware of languages and to promote multilingualism and the learning of languages by organising information and public-awareness projects. It has been possible to co-finance projects targeting a wide range of languages.

 

Question no 74 by Konstantinos Hatzidakis (H-0651/01)
 Subject: Progress with the national land register
 

Can the Commission report on the stage reached in its talks with the Greek Government on the problems which have been detected in the implementation of the land register programme in Greece under the second CSF, and can it say what the situation is likely to be with regard to the third CSF?

 
  
 

As the Commission has explained on various occasions when similar questions regarding the National Land Register in Greece have been put by the Honourable Member and by other Members of Parliament, since the beginning of the year the Commission has held a series of discussions and exchanges of letters with the Greek authorities on the subject of the project being co-financed by the European Regional Development Fund (ERDF). Those authorities, in reply to questions from the Commission, submitted voluminous files, in February and again in May, explaining their position with regard to the various points raised by the Commission.

This correspondence has been taking place in the context of the procedure laid down in article 24 of Council Regulation (EEC) No 2082/93(1) 1 laying down provisions for the implementation of the Structural Funds.

The Commission carried out an in-depth analysis of all the information provided by the Greek authorities. The conclusions of that analysis were sent to the Greek authorities in July, thus giving them an opportunity to inform the Commission of any new factors that might have arisen in the meantime or to correct any errors. We expect a reply from the Greek authorities by the end of September 2001.

On the basis of the file that was prepared and of any comments by the Greek Government, the Commission will take a final decision on this project, in particular on the question of whether to reduce or possibly cancel Community aid.

At the present moment, the National Land Register project in Greece is not part of any programme adopted by the Commission under the third Community Support Framework (CSF) and in particular the recently adopted Environment Programme. Consequently it is not eligible for co-financing under the third CSF.

When the present procedure finally comes to an end, then it will be necessary to decide whether, and if so subject to what conditions, the National Land Register project in Greece can once again become eligible for co-financing under the third CSF.

Question no 75 by Torben Lund (H-0652/01)
 Subject: Batteries
 

The 1991 Batteries Directive causes a lot of problems for the environment, partly because cadmium, a highly polluting substance, was not prohibited long ago. Will the Commission state whether a revision of the Batteries Directive is being prepared, in particular with a view to possibly phasing out cadmium, and whether it is waiting for further analyses before imposing a ban on cadmium, even though cadmium's highly damaging effects on health and the environment are clearly documented? Lastly, will the Commission also state whether, in connection with the possible introduction of a returns scheme for batteries, it has asked industry if it intends to cooperate in devising such a scheme?

 
  
 

A revision of the Batteries Directive is under preparation. As the results of a general and a targeted risk-assessment will only be available at a later stage, this proposal will in all probability not include a phasing out of cadmium. Instead, being aware of the problems caused by cadmium on human health and environment, mandatory collection targets and/or a deposit scheme are among the options currently considered.

Industry is being asked to comment on the option of a deposit scheme.

 

Question no 76 by Carlos Bautista Ojeda (H-0653/01)
 Subject: Illegal tree felling in the Sierra de Baza Nature Reserve
 

In 1998 and 1999 the riverside plant cover was cleared from the Sierra de Baza Nature Reserve. Poplar trees were thus cut down, harming the riverside vegetation as a whole and the ecosystem which this constituted. Reports by the Spanish nature conservation service, SEPRONA, indicate that nearly 14 000 trees were indiscriminately felled, although, according to the administrative permits granted by the authorities, only the dead wood was supposed to be removed and put to use. The illegal felling has created eutrophication problems. Ecosystems protected by Community legislation have likewise been damaged, in particular deciduous Mediterranean woodland, a natural habitat of Community interest. Protected species have been endangered. No environmental impact assessments have been carried out, nor have remedies been proposed. Investment in the nature reserve has been financed by EAGGF funds. Criminal proceedings have been instituted in Spain in connection with the actions described above.

Is the Commission aware of these facts and does it believe that Spain has infringed Directives 92/43/EEC(1) and 79/409/EEC(2) (the ‘habitats’ and ‘wild birds’ directives)? Does it know whether measures to restore the plant cover have been financed under the EAGGF or an environmental impact assessment has been carried out in accordance with Community legislation?

 
  
 

The Commission is not aware of the events described by the Honourable Member. However, it has confirmed that the area was proposed by Spain as a Site of Community Interest (SCI), ES 6140001 “Sierra de Baza”, for inclusion in the Natura 2000 Network, under the Habitats Directive 92/43/EEC.

The site in question covers an area of over 53 833 hectares. This area includes up to 18 different types of habitat covered by Annex I to the said Directive, of which five are priority habitats (6110, 7220, 6220, 9561, and 9533).

Felling activities have to be carried out by an approved plan, but in this case it appears that they cut down trees irrespective of the prescriptions.

Felling activities are not supported by the European Agricultural Guidance and Guarantee Fund (EAGGF), but the Honourable Member indicates that other activities for nature conservation purposes in these areas have been financed. This also does not exclude the felling of trees. On the same grounds EAGGF does not support actions of forest habitats restoration after damage. It provides only for actions that enhance the ecological functions of forests and not for restoration of damaged forest areas.

The Commission will be contacting the Spanish authorities to ask them what measures need to be implemented in order to comply with the Habitats Directive, in particular Article 6 thereof.

Once the Commission has received a reply, it will be able to decide on whether Community legislation has been complied with in this particular case.

 
 

(1) OJ L 206, 22.7.1992, p. 7.
(2) OJ L 103, 25.4.1979, p. 1.

 

Question no 77 by Willy C.E.H. De Clercq (H-0658/01)
 Subject: Trade in raw cattle hides
 

For several months, European dealers in raw cattle hides have been forbidden to export their products to markets in Asia, because of the situation with regard to foot and mouth disease. Although exports of meat and other food products have now been resumed – from Belgium as well as elsewhere – Asian markets remain closed to European dealers in raw cattle hides.

This ban on exports is bringing heavy pressure to bear on the European market in raw cattle hides because Italy is the only country to which all the other European countries can sell them. Moreover, Italy is taking advantage of the situation by negotiating very low prices. What is more, countries outside Europe and America are steadily taking over as suppliers for the whole of the Asian market, so that firstly European dealers are losing all their Asian customers and secondly the market is being flooded with hides.

Will the Commission take action to deal with this situation? If so, how, and can it do so soon, in view of the highly precarious situation facing European dealers in raw cattle hides? If not, why not?

 
  
 

The Commission is aware of the restrictions on imports which certain Asian countries have imposed regarding raw cattle hides originating from the Community. These restrictions were imposed following the outbreak of foot-and-mouth disease in “continental Europe” in March 2001.

This external problem has also had internal repercussions on trading relations between Member States. The surplus of hides which some Member States are having difficulty disposing of has put great pressure on their markets. This does not appear to be a universal problem within the Community. Some Member States are still suffering from a shortage of raw hides on their markets.

The Commission does not have any legal instrument that would allow it to intervene in the internal market for raw cattle hides. In other words, the market is governed by the laws of supply and demand.

On the other hand, as regards restrictions placed on imports by certain Asian countries, the Commission has successfully undertaken negotiations, both bilateral and multilateral, in the context of the World Trade Organisation (WTO), with a view to persuading third countries to apply European and international health standards.

The Commission has in this way succeeded in getting several measures restricting imports lifted, but there are still certain others in existence. Since the end of August, eleven Member States have been recognised as being free from foot-and-mouth disease under the rules of the International Office of Epizootics. Restrictions on imports of hides from the Community are therefore becoming increasingly unjustified. When restrictions are applied to hides coming from countries not affected by foot-and-mouth disease, they are no longer justified and must therefore be removed.

The Commission will continue its efforts at both bilateral and multilateral level and in particular within the WTO SPS Committee (Committee on Sanitary and Phytosanitary Measures).

 

Question no 78 by David W. Martin (H-0660/01)
 Subject: Primates
 

The biomedical Primate Research Centre, Rijswijk, Holland currently holds over 100 chimpanzees, the last great apes to be used for research in the EU. The Dutch Government has indicated that it will not license further experiments using these animals. What plans does the Commission have for introducing legislation that will end, once and for all, the use of great apes in research?

 
  
 

The use of non-human primates in research, and great apes in particular, is a sensitive matter. The Commission fully supports efforts to reduce the number of non-human primates used as laboratory animals and agrees that the use of great apes in research should be looked into as a matter of urgency.

It has further become clear that Directive 86/609/EEC on the protection of animals used for experimental and other scientific purposes does not provide sufficient controls to safeguard the welfare of these sensitive species. Therefore the Commission has taken stock of the current situation in the use of non-human primates in experiments in the Community. Discussions with the Member States, industry and non-governmental organisations in the field of animal welfare have already started on the issues where further attention and controls are needed.

The Commission is preparing a revision of Directive 86/609/EEC. This revision will incorporate also initiatives in the area of the use of non-human primates, not only in research, but also in other scientific procedures. Among these issues, the question of banning the use of great apes will be thoroughly examined.

 

Question no 79 by Glenys Kinnock (H-0661/01)
 Subject: Gender training
 

What plans does the Commission have to introduce compulsory periodic pre-posting gender training for Commission officials at all levels who are seconded to delegations?

Does the Commission agree that such training should cover gender awareness, including attitudes and sensitivities regarding gender relations, and training in gender planning, i.e. the more technical gender analysis of programme design and implementation?

 
  
 

For several years the Commission has organised pre-posting training courses that are compulsory for all officials who will be posted to the delegations.

The current pre-posting training provides the officials with the necessary information on the role of delegations and multicultural management, diplomatic practice and protocol. Diversity issues are widely discussed, because of the multicultural composition of the staff and the working environment in the delegations.

Spouses and partners are also invited to participate in the pre-posting training.

The Commission sees the gender issue as very relevant in the external service. In the administrative culture of the delegations there are a lot of sensitivities – gender issue being one of them – that have to be dealt with carefully. Training is a good instrument in enhancing the change.

The Commission will set up a new training policy for the external service by the end of the year. In this context the gender issue will also be dealt with.

 

Question no 80 by Paul Rübig (H-0662/01)
 Subject: Restriction of the right to deduct input tax
 

After 1.1.1978, but prior to its accession to the European Union on 1.1.1995, Austria still imposed various retroactive restrictions on the previously granted right to deduct input tax.

For instance, pursuant to Article 12(2)(2)(a) of the Law on Turnover Tax, following the 1988 amendment to that law (BGBl 410/88), the portion of the purchase cost of a motor car used as a hotel vehicle exceeding AS 467 000 (EUR 33 938.21) is not deductible as operating expenditure, and taxes already paid in respect thereof are not deductible. This also constitutes discrimination against certain higher medium-class vehicles.

In the light of the European Court of Justice's decisions in joined cases C-177/99 and C-181/99 (Ampafrance) and in cases C-345/99 and C-40/00 (Commission v France), are such retroactive restrictions on the right of deduction in respect of what is clearly business or professional expenditure covered by Article 17(6) of Directive 77/388/EEC(1)?

Does this unobjective non-deductibility in the case of income and corporation tax, owing to discrimination against certain higher medium-class vehicles, infringe EC law?

 
  
 

1. According to the Honourable Member’s question, Austria introduced certain retroactive restrictions on the right to deduct VAT after 1 January 1978 but before Austria’s accession to the Union.

Regarding the question of whether those restrictions are covered by Article 17(6) of the Sixth VAT Directive, the Commission points out that, in the case of Austria, that provision did not come into force until the date of its accession, in other words 1 January 1995. Therefore the national exclusions from the right to deduct, which predate 1 January 1995, are not incompatible with Article 17(6) of the Sixth VAT Directive.

2. The fact that the deductibility of the costs of certain vehicles is also restricted in the context of the taxation of incomes does not in itself constitute an infringement of Community tax law.

 
 

(1) OJ L 145, 13.6.1977, p. 1.

 

Question no 81 by Anne Elisabet Jensen (H-0664/01)
 Subject: Directive on part-time work
 

Does the Commission take the view that the directive on part-time work imposes a requirement to lower the national limit on the number of hours worked from 15 to 8 hours, or would the current 15-hour limit be compatible with Article 141 of the Treaty concerning equality?

The part-time work directive clearly makes it possible for pay and other entitlements to be based on the number of hours worked (the pro rata temporis principle) in order to ensure equal treatment. In some cases, however, benefits are accrued only through longer-term employment relationships, possibly because jobs in which fewer hours are worked typically do not possess the confidential nature that is an essential feature of salaried work. Nevertheless, the Danish Ministry of Labour takes the view that the limit on the number of hours worked laid down in the Salaried Employees Act has to be lowered from 15 to 8 hours so that Denmark meets the requirements of the part-time work directive. As a result, young people and students working in the retail sector will be obliged to work for two months from the time they give notice of termination of their employment contract.

 
  
 

The Commission would refer the Honourable Member to its answer to her written question P-1741/01.

 

Question no 82 by Paulo Casaca (H-0665/01)
 Subject: Protection of the rights of Community workers outwith their country of origin
 

The Portuguese press is carrying increasing numbers of stories of how the rights of Portuguese workers recruited to work in other Member States are being flagrantly violated in respect of either Portuguese or the other countries’ legislation.

Particularly striking is the case of workers recruited by the ‘Atlanco’ agency to work in Northern Ireland, where there have been instances of non-existent medical assistance, deductions to cover compulsory social security contributions, and failure to observe minimum wages on the holiday rights.

Given the scale and importance of the problem, does the Commission not think that the legal situation needs to be clarified and the requisite European level inspection activities put in hand to ensure that the right to freedom of movement, social legislation and the principle of non-discrimination are respected?

 
  
 

The Commission would like to draw the attention of the Honourable Member to the fact that Community law – notably Regulations 1408/71 and 574/72(1) 1 - protects the social security rights of migrant workers by co-ordinating the different national social security schemes of Member States. One of the main principles of this co-ordination is to determine the social security legislation which is applicable to a migrant worker. As a rule, such a worker is subject to the social security legislation of the Member State where he or she is employed. Therefore, if a person works in Northern Ireland he or she is principally subject to the social security legislation of the United Kingdom. But the Honourable Member seems to refer to an important exception to this rule which is contained in Regulation 1408/71, namely the one relating to the posting of workers. A posted worker is normally employed by an employer in one Member State, but the employer sends him or her to another Member State to perform work there for a limited period of time. Under these circumstances the posted worker remains subject to the social security legislation of the sending Member State and contributions must continue to be paid to the social security scheme of this Member State. Nevertheless, the posted worker is entitled to health care in the Member State of employment on behalf of the Member State to which contributions are paid.

Although Community law determines which social security legislation is applicable, Member States remain free to determine both the conditions concerning the right or duty to be insured with their social security scheme (e.g. as concerns coverage for health care) and the conditions for entitlement to benefits provided that these do not discriminate between their own nationals and those of other Member States. It then remains for each Member State whose social security legislation has been determined to be applicable to ensure that its respective legislation, e.g. concerning the payment of social security contributions, is complied with. It should furthermore be stressed that the Commission can only ensure proper application of Community law in the field of social security by governments of Member States and does not act as inspector in the social field.

As regards minimum wages and holiday rights, which are working conditions, the rules of Directive 96/71/EC of the Parliament and of the Council of 16 December 1996 concerning the posting of workers in the framework of the provision of services(2) 1, must be observed if the agency is a temporary employment undertaking or placement agency hiring out workers to a user undertaking in Northern Ireland, provided that there is an employment relationship between, in this case, the Portuguese company and the posted workers.

In the situations covered by the Directive, the Member States are required to ensure that, whatever the law applicable to the employment relationship, the undertakings concerned guarantee posted workers certain terms and conditions of employment in force in the host country. These terms and conditions - considered mandatory rules - include in particular minimum rates of pay and minimum paid annual holidays. Where such mandatory rules are determined by the law of the host country, the Directive covers all sectors of activity. Where the terms and conditions of employment are laid down by collective agreements which have been declared universally applicable, the Directive applies to activities relating to construction.

 
 

(1)1 Regulation (EEC) No 1408/71 of the Council of 14 June 1971 on the application of social security schemes to employed persons and their families moving within the Community - OJ L 149 of 5.7.1971
Regulation (EEC) No 574/72 of the Council of 21 March 1972 fixing the procedure for implementing Regulation (EEC) No 1408/71 on the application of social security schemes to employed persons and their families moving within the Community – OJ L 74 of 27.03.1972

Council Regulation (EC) No 118/97 of 2 December 1996 amending and updating Regulations (EEC) No 1408/71 and (EEC) No 574/72 – OJ L 28 of 30.1.1997
(2)1 OJ L 18 of 21.1.1997

Question no 86 by Astrid Thors (H-0672/01)

The EUROJUS network is currently a network of lawyers working at the Commission’s offices in the Member States and helping citizens to obtain justice in connection with complaints relating to Community law. Their work provides important information on the application of Community law.
