Within the Commission, DG Press is responsible for the EUROJUS network which is managed decentrally by the Commission Representations in the Member States.

Ten of the Representations of the Commission have a EUROJUS staff. Wherever no Eurojus service is provided, the Representations themselves answer the citizens’ questions or direct them to the relevant services.

The EUROJUS network has been in existence for fifteen years. It was, therefore, decided that a substantive review into its operations should be undertaken, taking into account the quality and effectiveness of the service provided. A review at this stage is especially important in view of the new orientation decided by the Commission in the Communication “ a new framework for co-operation on activities concerning the information and communication policy of the European Union” - adopted on 27 June 2001 - and also in view of enlargement.

The Parliament will be informed as soon as the review has been completed and a final decision can be taken.

 

Question no 87 by Maurizio Turco (H-0673/01)
 Subject: Clarification of answer to Oral Question H-0392/01 on EU cooperation with UNICDP
 

Given that the information given by the Commission in its answer to Oral Question H-0392/01(1) to the effect that the UN internal report had exonerated Mr Arlacchi has proved to be inaccurate, should the Commission not review its information sources?

Since the report actually refers to over-centralized, arbitrary and untransparent management, waste of resources, lack of procedures for review and approval of projects, lack of objective quality control and fund-raising strategies with no long-term prospects, does the Commission not think it should adopt a clear position and take action commensurate with the gravity of the situation?

 
  
 

The malfunctioning of the United Nations International Drug Control programme (UNDCP) has already been the subject of a position adopted by the Commission and set out in the answer supplied by the Commission to Oral Question H-0392/01 at Parliament’s plenary session of May 2001.

Following the serious allegations concerning the operating methods of the UNDCP, an investigation was carried out, which resulted in two reports by the services of the United Nations Organisation (UNO).

Following a detailed analysis, it seems that the Board of Auditors and the Office of Internal Oversight Services (OIOS) identified certain practices that were subject to criticism, and in particular they criticised a management style which was too personalised, deficiencies in budget programming, poor management of human resources (especially at headquarters) and mediocre implementation of worldwide programmes. The reports in no way confirm the existence of fraud or the misuse of funds.

These criticisms have given rise to concern on the part of states which grant non-targeted subsidies to the UNDCP and which participate directly in funding the headquarters or certain worldwide projects. Those states are not involved in either the identification or the implementation of the projects.

Those states also second experts to the UNDCP, and they expect those experts to be able to work in good conditions and have real responsibilities.

As far as the Commission is concerned, although the Council Regulation makes provision for a lump-sum contribution to the UNDCP, there has been no Community financing destined for the worldwide programmes or the UNDCP headquarters. Funding is granted solely on the basis of projects which are sometimes assessed by the Commission. Before approval, such projects are examined in accordance with normal procedures and are referred to the Financing Committees. The Commission then monitors their implementation.

Finally, the Commission does not second any experts to the UNDCP.

However, the Commission has decided to conduct an audit of each of the current projects. Like the majority of the Member States, the Commission does not envisage any change in its position, since it does not have at its disposal any new information that would justify such a change. In the context of the Major Donor Group, the Commission is participating in the discussions on how to improve the effectiveness of the UNDCP.

 
 

(1) Written answer of 15.5.2001

 

Question no 88 by Mark Francis Watts (H-0674/01)
 Subject: Valwo Zoo, Central Spain
 

An elephant known as Rhanee is being kept at Valwo Zoo in central Spain, alone and in a pen the size of a tennis court with no shelter or bathing water. This animal has already suffered a life of considerable abuse and cruelty, some of which, sadly, was inflicted in the United Kingdom. The UK-based pressure group Animal Defenders has offered to arrange for the elephant to be transferred to the Performing Animal Welfare Sanctuary in California where she can live out the expected 30 years that remain of her life in an environment where she will receive expert care. In the interests of animal welfare, will the Commission join me in calling on the Spanish authorities to press the owners of the Valwo Zoo (Parques Reunidos) to agree to the elephant being transferred?

 
  
 

According to the "Protocol on the Protection and Welfare of Animals", which is annexed to the Treaty of Amsterdam, the European Institutions have to pay full regard to the welfare requirements of animals when formulating and implementing Community legislation.

The Commission is taking full consideration of animal welfare issues, among others, by ensuring high standards for the welfare of animals on the farm, during transport and at slaughter, by improving the protection of wild animals by regulating trade therein and the protection of animals used for scientific purposes, and it is taking into account animal welfare concerns in its technical legislation.

Nevertheless, the competencies of the Commission are limited and Member States remain generally responsible for matters of cruelty against animals, such as mistreatment and the use of animals in competitions or shows.

Council Directive 1999/22/EC of 29 March 1999 relating to the keeping of wild animals in zoos aims to improve the situation of wild animals kept in zoos. In particular, Article 3, 3rd indent, specifies the requirements on zoos regarding accommodation and husbandry. However, the provisions of the Directive are not yet applicable, as the deadline for transposition of the Directive is 9 April 2002. Once it is in force, the responsibility for enforcing the national provisions adopted pursuant to the Directive lies with the Member States.

Thus, even if the Commission has no means to call on the Spanish authorities regarding the transfer of the elephant from Spain to California, it expects that its initiatives will favour it.

Question no 90 by Efstratios Korakas (H-0679/01)
 Subject: Studies on the use of depleted uranium shells
 

Mr Kinnock has said that reputable scientific studies show that the use of depleted uranium shells poses no threat to human health or the environment, despite the fact that fresh evidence to the contrary appears with every passing day; one example is the document by the US Department of Defense of August 1993 which states that the expected consequences of exposure of human beings to depleted uranium dust is an increased incidence of lung and bone cancer.

By letter of 13 February 2001 I asked Mr Kinnock to forward these studies to me. In his reply he said he was forwarding my request to the Commissioner responsible, Mrs Wallström, but so far - five months later - I have still not received any reply or any study.

Will the Commission say whether these studies really exist and when it intends to forward them to me?

 
  
 

In the Commission’s reply in the context of Oral Question H-0055/01 by Mrs. Kratsa-Tsagaropoulou given during Question Time of the February 2001 plenary session of the Parliament, Commissioner Kinnock confirmed that there was well documented scientific consensus that the impact of test firing of depleted uranium (DU) shells in marine waters would be minimal compared to the ubiquitous natural occurrence of uranium.

The Honourable Member subsequently requested transmission of the studies that the Commission would have referred to. In fact the Commission did not have in mind any specific document on the issue but rather the above-mentioned scientific consensus.

Few days later the Commission received the opinion of the Group of Experts established under Article 31 of the Euratom Treaty. This document was immediately released to the press, was available on the EUROPA web-site, and raised a wide interest at international level.

While the Experts dealt essentially with general uses of DU, against the background of military uses and the consequences DU shells impacting on terrestrial targets, the document also contains a paragraph (53) reading as follows:

“Test firing is being carried out in the sea. Due to the low corrosion rate and to the large volume of surrounding seawater, no detectable increase of uranium concentration in seawater or biota is expected.”

The Experts thus fully confirmed the position taken by the Commission.

The Commission nevertheless regrets that no direct reply was given to the request for information from the Honourable Member of the Parliament.

 

Question no 91 by Antonios Trakatellis (H-0681/01)
 Subject: The Thessaloniki underground: delay in the execution of the work - negotiations with the EIB - charges of infringement of Community law
 

Twenty-nine months after signature and ratification by the Greek Parliament of the transfer document for the Thessaloniki underground, negotiations are continuing for the conclusion of loan agreements; moreover the Commission representative told the European Parliament's Committee on Petitions on 9 July that in view of the risk that the transfer agreement would be declared void, written authorisation for extending the negotiations had been given twice and that the matter remained in suspension as the financial agreements between the contractor and the lenders had not been finalised and the works themselves therefore could not begin.

What is the present state of affairs with the Thessaloniki underground and why were the financial agreements between the contractor and the Greek Government and the lenders not finalised so that the Commission could proceed to scrutinise and where appropriate endorse the compatibility of the agreements in question with the rules on state aid and to adopt a decision on the charges of infringements of Community rules on public contracts? As the delays have meant that the project cannot be carried out under the second CSF and as the handling of the matter by the Commission and the EIB is required to be in accordance with the treaties, Community law and the Community's interests, how will the Commission and the EIB safeguard the Community's interests in the timely execution of the project during the term of the third CSF for Greece?

 
  
 

Having already replied to questions in the same vein a number of times in the past few months and having presented in June and July its position in the petitions Committee, the Commission regrets that it is not in a position to add a great deal today to the information that has already been given to the Parliament.

According to the principles of subsidiarity and the methods which have been established for the implementation of the Structural Fund regulations the Commission can take no responsibility for the conduct of negotiations between the concessionaire of a particular project and those bodies from whom the concessionaire intends to borrow money. Whilst it may well be true that protracted negotiations could have a negative effect on the timely completion of a project and thus its financing through the Community support framework, the Commission would emphasise that it is the responsibility of the Member State to ensure that projects are undertaken according to the timetable laid out in its initial proposals.

The Commission will of course follow developments in this case closely. It will not fail to act as and when it feels this is appropriate to do so.

 

Question no 92 by Karin Riis-Jørgensen (H-0682/01)
 Subject: Failure to implement properly parts of Directive 92/46/EEC in Denmark
 

Further to the reply to question H-0364/01(1) and question P-1794/01 to the Commission concerning failure to implement properly parts of Directive 92/46/EEC(2) in Denmark, can the Commission provide the following information:

Does it consider that it is acceptable under existing case law that Denmark has not yet introduced statutory health requirements regarding the production and marketing of raw milk for human consumption in accordance with those set out in paragraphs 1-3 of Article 4 of Directive 92/46/EEC?

Further, does the Commission consider it acceptable under existing case law that Denmark has not introduced statutory health requirements for the production and marketing of all milk-based products obtained from raw milk in accordance with Articles 6 and 7 of Directive 92/46/EEC?

 
  
 

Denmark does not have any legislation on the production and marketing of raw milk intended for human consumption or of products based on raw milk, since the latter are prohibited.

Under Article 249 (ex Article 189) of the EC Treaty, a directive is binding, as to the result to be achieved, upon each Member State to which it is addressed, but it leaves the national authorities to decide upon the form and methods.

As the Court of Justice has regularly confirmed, the transposition of a directive into national law does not necessarily require the provisions being transposed to be reproduced formally and word-for-word in a specific national provision. However, it is essential that the restrictive measures contained in a directive should be included in a national act in such a way as to create a legal context which will suffice to ensure that the measures are applied.

Consistent judgments by the Court also show that in cases where a directive lays down optional provisions or does not place a specific area entirely under regulation, the Member State has the right, within the limits of the general principles of the EC Treaty, either not to transpose the optional part of the directive or to retain or create national legislation to supplement the Community provisions.

In the case referred to by the Honourable Member, and with reference to the provisions of Council Directive 92/46/EEC of 16 June 1992, it is necessary to point out that Community legislation has not laid down any instructions regarding health, except in cases where a Member State wishes to permit the use of raw milk.

The result, as the Commission has already stated in its replies to Oral Question H-0364/01 during Question Time at the plenary session of Parliament in May 2001 and written question P-1794/01 put by the Honourable Member, is that the requirements laid down in Article 4 of Directive 92/46/EEC are only applicable in cases where a Member State authorises the placing on the market of milk intended for direct human consumption, whereas under Article 7(A)(9), each Member State has to lay down its own health rules for milk-based products which are not made from heat-treated milk or which have not been subjected to any heat treatment during their manufacturing process, and those rules must be sufficient to enable such products to meet the health criteria guaranteed for every finished product.

 
 

(1) Written reply of 15.5.2001.
(2) OJ L 268, 14.9.1992, p. 1.

 

Question no 93 by David Robert Bowe (H-0686/01)
 Subject: Transfer of manufacturing facilities to Central Europe
 

What powers does the Commission have to examine the circumstances in which EU-based manufacturers transfer their component manufacturing facilities to Central and Eastern European countries while maintaining final assembly of the goods in the EU, labelling the goods as having been manufactured in the EU?

 
  
 

Owing to the lack of consensus within Community industry, there are no harmonised rules at Community level on indication of the origin on products marketed in the Community, as this is a matter for the national legislation of the Member States. Where such national regulations exist, they generally refer to the non-preferential origin of products in accordance with the World Trade Organisation Agreement on rules of origin. Under the Community Customs Code(1), goods are deemed to originate in a country if they are wholly obtained or produced in that country or, where several countries are involved, if they underwent their last, 'substantial', processing or working in that country. In the very general case described by the Honourable Member, assembly operations in the Community of imported components would therefore have to be of a substantial nature for the finished product to be deemed to originate in the Community.

The Commission could not be called upon to intervene in this matter except where non-compliance with this rule for acquiring Community origin is likely to have an impact on the proper application of Community provisions. This is not the case where the impact is confined to the implementation of purely national provisions. In any event, European producers also have other instruments enabling them, on the one hand, to protect certain geographical indications and, on the other, to inform consumers about the origin of their products(2).

 
 

(1) Council Regulation (EEC) No 2913/92 of 12 October 1992 (OJ L 302, 19.10.1992).
(2) Council Directive 89/104/EEC of 21 December 1988 to approximate the laws of the Member States relating to trade marks (OJ L 40, 11.2.1989); Council Regulation (EC) No 40/94 of 20 December 1993 on the Community trade mark (OJ L 11, 14.1.1994); Council Directive 89/109/EEC of 21 December 1988 on the approximation of the laws of the Member States relating to materials and articles intended to come into contact with foodstuffs (OJ L 40, 11.2.1989).

 

Question no 94 by Hans-Peter Martin (H-0688/01)
 Subject: Future of the European telecommunications industry following the EP's rejection of the Lehne report
 

Following Parliament's rejection on 4 July 2001 of the directive on takeovers (Lehne report), what implications do you foresee for mergers between leading European telecommunications companies? Will the rejection of the directive foster or hinder the developments vital to the industry, and why?

 
  
 

The Commission regrets the rejection by the Parliament of the Proposal for a Directive on Take over bids. Although this Proposal did not intend to promote the take over bid technique as the only or preferable technique for corporate restructuring, it is clear that the continued absence of common rules for take over bids makes corporate acquisitions more difficult. This is particularly so because of the possibility for an offered company in the case of a hostile take over bid to continue applying defensive measures aimed at frustrating the bid. The Commission has therefore announced that it will come back with a new proposal as soon as possible.

Given the extent of the corporate restructuring currently taking place among European telecommunications companies, it may be that the telecommunications sector will be one of the sectors most affected by the absence of common rules on take-over bids, but in itself rejection of the Proposed Directive is unlikely to affect the pace of restructuring in this sector.
